<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-congenital-talipes">Management of Congenital Talipes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-congenital-talipes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>progressive casting during the first months of life</li>
<li>a special brace that the child wears for two or more months</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="surgery">Surgery<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#surgery" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Soft tissue surgery that releases the tight tissues around the joints and results in lengthening of tendons so the foot can assume a more corrected position</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bony procedures such as “breaking bone” and resetting the bone to correct deformities, or fusing joints together to stabilize joints to enable the bones to grow solidly together.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Tendon transfers to move the tendons to a different position, so they can move the foot into a corrected position.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nursing management focuses on</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pre and post operative management of the patient</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Management of a patient on PoP for a long period</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Patient/client education on the condition, its management and expected outcomes</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="osteogenesis-imperfecta">Osteogenesis Imperfecta<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osteogenesis-imperfecta" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Osteogenesis imperfecta (OI) is an inherited (genetic) bone disorder that is present at birth. It is also known as brittle bone disease. A child born with OI may have soft bones that break (fracture) easily, bones that are not formed normally, and other problems. Signs and symptoms may range from mild to severe.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="there-are-at-least-8-different-types-of-the-disease">There are at least 8 different types of the disease.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#there-are-at-least-8-different-types-of-the-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Type I. Mildest and most common type. About 50% of all affected children have this type. There are few fractures and deformities</li>
<li>Type II. Most severe type. A baby has very short arms and legs, a small chest, and soft skull. He or she may be born with fractured bones. He or she may also have a low birth weight and lungs that are not well developed. A baby with type II OI usually dies within weeks of birth</li>
<li>Type III. Most severe type in babies who don't die as newborns. At birth, a baby may have slightly shorter arms and legs than normal and arm, leg, and rib fractures. A baby may also have a larger than normal head, a triangle-shaped face, a deformed chest and spine, and breathing and swallowing problems. These symptoms are different in each baby.</li>
<li>Type IV. Symptoms are between mild and severe. A baby with type IV may be diagnosed at birth. He or she may not have any fractures until crawling or walking. The bones of the arms and legs may not be straight. He or she may not grow normally.</li>
<li>Type V. Similar to type IV. Symptoms may be medium to severe. It is common to have enlarged thickened areas (hypertrophic calluses) in the areas where large bones are fractured</li>
<li>Type VI. Very rare. Symptoms are medium. Similar to type IV.</li>
<li>Type VII. May be like type IV or type II. It is common to have shorter than normal height. Also common to have shorter than normal upper arm and thighbones.</li>
<li>Type VIII. Similar to types II and III. Very soft bones and severe growth problems.</li>
</ul>
<p>What causes osteogenesis imperfecta in a child?</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>OI is passed on through the genes. The different types are passed on in different ways. The gene may be inherited from one or both parents. Or the gene can be passed on from an unexplained change (spontaneous mutation) of a gene.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Most babies with OI have a defect of one of two genes. These genes help in forming collagen. Collagen is a main part of connective tissue that connects and supports the whole body, including the bones. Because of the defect, there is not enough collagen. Or the collagen is abnormal.</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>What are the symptoms of osteogenesis imperfecta in a child?</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Easily broken bones</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bone deformities, such as bowing of the legs</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Discoloration of the white of the eye (sclera), may be blue or gray in color</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A barrel-shaped chest</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A curved spine</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A triangle-shaped face</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Loose joints</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Muscle weakness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Skin that easily bruises</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hearing loss in early adulthood</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Soft, discolored teeth</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>How is osteogenesis imperfecta diagnosed in a child?</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>X-rays. These may show many changes such as weak or deformed bones and fractures.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Lab tests. Blood, saliva, and skin may be checked. The tests may include gene testing.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dual Energy X-ray Absorptiometry scan (DXA or DEXA scan). A scan of the bones to check for softening.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bone biopsy. A sample of the hipbone is checked. This test requires sleep medicine (general anesthesia).</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-is-osteogenesis-imperfecta-treated-in-a-child">How is osteogenesis imperfecta treated in a child?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-is-osteogenesis-imperfecta-treated-in-a-child" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The main goal of treatment is to prevent deformities and fractures. And, once your child gets older, to allow him or her to function as independently as possible. Treatments for preventing or correcting symptoms may include:</li>
<li>Bisphosphonate medicines. These are medicines that help to strengthen bones and prevent fractures. They may be used in most types of OI. They may be given by mouth or by IV (intravenous line) into a vein.</li>
<li>Care of fractures. The lightest possible materials are used to cast fractured bones. To prevent further problems, it is recommended that a child begin moving or using the affected area as soon as possible.</li>
<li>Orthopedic treatment. May include bracing and splinting. Surgery may also be needed.</li>
<li>Rodding. Metal rods are inserted to help hold in place (stabilize) and prevent deformities of long bones.</li>
<li>Dental procedures. Treatments, including capping teeth, braces, and surgery may be needed.</li>
<li>Physical and occupational therapy. Both are very important in babies and children with OI.</li>
<li>Assistive devices. Wheelchairs and other custom-made equipment may be needed as babies get older.</li>
</ul>
<p>Nursing interventions</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Support limbs, do not pull on arms or legs or lift the legs to prevent more fractures or deformities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Position the patient with care.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Check the patient’s circulatory, motor, and sensory abilities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Provide emergency care of fractures.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Observe for signs of compartment syndrome.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Encourage diet high in protein and vitamins to promote healing.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Encourage fluids to prevent constipation, renal calculi, and urinary tract infection.</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>Nursing interventions continued</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Provide care for client with traction, with cast, or with open reduction.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Encourage mobility when possible.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Administer analgesics as prescribed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Teach the patient preventive measures.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Monitor hearing needs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Aggressively treat all upper respiratory infections including colds</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>What are the possible complications of osteogenesis imperfecta in a child?</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Complications may affect most body systems in a baby or child with OI. The risk of developing complications depends on the type and severity of your baby’s OI. Complications may include the following:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Respiratory infections, such as pneumonia</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Heart problems such as poor heart valve function</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Kidney stones</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Joint problems</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hearing loss</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Eye conditions and vision loss</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="hip-dysplasia">Hip dysplasia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hip-dysplasia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>A spectrum of disorders related to abnormal development of the hip.</li>
<li>Develops at any time during fetal life, infancy, or childhood</li>
<li>There is a shallow acetabulum, subluxation, or dislocation (affects Head of the femur or Acetabulum or Surrounding capsules and soft tissues)</li>
<li>Incidence: 10 per 1000 live births and 80% are female</li>
</ul>
<p>What are hip dysplasia symptoms?</p>
<p>• The most common hip dysplasia symptoms include:</p>
<p>• Hip pain (usually in the front of your groin).</p>
<p>• Feeling like your hip is loose or unstable.</p>
<p>• Limping when you walk or move.</p>
<p>• Having legs that are different lengths</p>
<p>Babies born with hip dysplasia that aren’t old enough to walk yet may have different hip dysplasia symptoms, including:</p>
<p>• Having one leg that turns out (away from the center of their body) more than the other.</p>
<p>• Moving one leg less than the other.</p>
<p>• Uneven or wrinkled skin around their thighs and butt (buttocks).</p>
<p>NB:At what age do babies with hip dysplasia show symptoms?</p>
<ul>
<li>Most babies with hip dysplasia are diagnosed when they’re younger than 6 months. Your healthcare provider might even diagnose your child right after they’re born.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Aetiological factors</li>
<li>Cause unknown but associated with</li>
<li>Family history</li>
<li>intrauterine position</li>
<li>breech delivery</li>
<li>joint laxity</li>
<li>postnatal positioning (swaddling)</li>
<li>Neurological disorders like in meningo-myelocele</li>
<li>physiologic (maternal hormone secretion)</li>
<li>mechanical (intrauterine posture pelvic laxity medication)</li>
<li>genetic.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis-and-tests">Diagnosis and Tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis-and-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>How is hip dysplasia diagnosed?</li>
<li>A healthcare provider will diagnose hip dysplasia with a physical exam and some imaging tests.</li>
<li>Ultrasound.</li>
<li>Hip X-rays.</li>
<li>A computed tomography (CT) scan</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ortolani-and-barlow-tests">Ortolani and Barlow tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ortolani-and-barlow-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Ortolani</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is performed by an examiner first flexing the hips and knees of a supine infant to 90 degrees, then with the examiner's index fingers placing anterior pressure on the greater trochanters, gently and smoothly abducting the infant's legs using the examiner's thumbs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A positive sign is a distinctive 'clunk' which can be heard and felt as the femoral head relocates anteriorly into the acetabulum</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Barlow</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The maneuver is easily performed by adducting the hip (bringing the thigh towards the midline) while applying light pressure on the knee, directing the force posteriorly. If the hip is dislocatable - that is, if the hip can be popped out of socket with this maneuver - the test is considered positives</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-and-treatment">Management and Treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-and-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-is-hip-dysplasia-treated">How is hip dysplasia treated?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-is-hip-dysplasia-treated" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>Physical therapy</strong>: A <strong>physical therapist</strong> will give you stretches and exercises to strengthen the muscles around your hip joint and improve your flexibility.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Hip dysplasia surgery</strong>: Your provider may recommend surgery to correct hip dysplasia if other treatments don’t work. The most common procedures to repair hip dysplasia are hip osteotomy and hip arthroscopies. <strong>Osteotomy</strong> is surgery to realign and reshape your bones. A <strong>hip arthroscopy</strong> is a minimally invasive technique to repair damage inside your hip joint. People with severe hip dysplasia and arthritis might need a <strong>hip replacement (arthroplasty)</strong>.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Wearing a brace</strong>: Babies may need to wear a brace or harness that holds their hips in place. This will keep their joints in proper alignment while their body grows and develops. Bracing is usually all your child will need to correct dysplasia, especially if they start treatment when they’re younger than 6 months old. Most babies need to wear a brace for a few months.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="disorders-of-the-spine">DISORDERS OF THE SPINE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disorders-of-the-spine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>INTERVERTABRAL DISC PROLAPSE</li>
<li>A prolapsed (herniated) disc occurs when the outer fibres of the intervertebral disc are injured, and the soft material known as the nucleus pulposus, ruptures out of its enclosed space.</li>
<li>In about 95% of all disc herniation cases, the L4-L5 or L5-S1 disc levels are involved.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-8b245e0c.jpeg" alt="img-112.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-2c24ad25.jpeg" alt="img-113.jpeg">
Herniated disc</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-3b9aeff8.jpeg" alt="img-114.jpeg">
Compressed lumbar spinal nerve</p>
<p>ADAM</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="causes-of-lumbar-disc-herniation">Causes of lumbar disc herniation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes-of-lumbar-disc-herniation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Trauma or injury to the disc</li>
<li>Disc degeneration</li>
<li>Congenital predisposition</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Risk factors</li>
<li>Factors that can increase the risk of a herniated disk include:</li>
<li>Weight. Excess body weight causes extra stress on the disks in the lower back.</li>
<li>Occupation. People with physically demanding jobs have a greater risk of back problems. Repetitive lifting, pulling, pushing, bending sideways and twisting also can increase the risk of a herniated disk.</li>
<li>Genetics. Some people inherit a predisposition to developing a herniated disk.</li>
<li>Smoking. It's thought that smoking lessens the oxygen supply to disks, causing them to break down more quickly.</li>
<li>Frequent driving. Being seated for long periods combined with the vibration from a motor vehicle engine can put pressure on the spine.</li>
<li>Being sedentary. Regular exercise can help prevent a herniated disk.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="types-of-disc-herniation">Types of disc herniation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-disc-herniation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are three types of disc herniation</p>
<ol>
<li>Protrusion / bulge</li>
<li>Disc herniation</li>
<li>Sequestration (disc rupture)</li>
</ol>
<p>A.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-8ff6b349.jpeg" alt="img-115.jpeg"></p>
<p>B.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-944ead3b.jpeg" alt="img-116.jpeg"></p>
<p>C.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-448795df.jpeg" alt="img-117.jpeg"></p>
<p>Disc Bulge (A)</p>
<p>Herniated Disc (B)</p>
<p>Disk Rupture (C)</p>
<p>symptoms</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Most herniated disks occur in the lower back, but they also can occur in the neck. Symptoms depend on where the disk is sitting and whether the disk is pressing on a nerve. Herniated disks often affect one side of the body.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Arm or leg pain. If your herniated disk is in your lower back, you'll typically feel pain in your lower back, buttocks, thigh and calf. You might have pain in part of your foot as well.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>For a herniated disk in your neck, you'll typically feel the most pain in your shoulder and arm. This pain might shoot into your arm or leg when you cough, sneeze or move into certain positions. Pain is often described as sharp or burning.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Numbness or tingling. People who have herniated disks often have radiating numbness or tingling in the body part served by the affected nerves.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Weakness. Muscles served by the affected nerves tend to weaken. This can cause you to stumble or affect your ability to lift or hold items.</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>Complications</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Worsening symptoms. Pain, numbness or weakness can increase to the point that they hamper your daily activities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bladder or bowel dysfunction. Cauda equina syndrome can cause incontinence or trouble urinating even with a full bladder.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Saddle anesthesia. This progressive loss of sensation affects the areas that would touch a saddle — the inner thighs, the backs of the legs and the area around the rectum.</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>Prevention</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>To help prevent a herniated disk, do the following:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Exercise. Strengthening the trunk muscles stabilizes and supports the spine.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maintain good posture. This reduces pressure on your spine and disks. Keep your back straight and aligned, particularly when sitting for long periods. Lift heavy objects properly, making your legs — not your back — do most of the work.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maintain a healthy weight. Excess weight puts more pressure on the spine and disks, making them more susceptible to herniation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Quit smoking. Avoid the use of any tobacco products.</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pelvic-inflammatory-disease">Pelvic inflammatory disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pelvic-inflammatory-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Pelvic inflammatory disease (PID) is an infection of one or more of the upper reproductive organs, including the uterus, fallopian tubes and ovaries. Untreated can cause scar tissue and pockets of infected fluid (abscesses) to develop in the reproductive tract, which can cause permanent damage.</li>
<li>It most often occurs when sexually transmitted bacteria spread from your vagina to your uterus, fallopian tubes or ovaries.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="symptoms">Symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The signs and symptoms of pelvic inflammatory disease might be mild and difficult to recognize. Some women don't have any signs or symptoms. When signs and symptoms of pelvic inflammatory disease (PID) are present, they most often include:</li>
<li>Pain — ranging from mild to severe — in your lower abdomen and pelvis</li>
<li>Unusual or heavy vaginal discharge that may have an unpleasant odor</li>
<li>Unusual bleeding from the vagina, especially during or after sex, or between periods</li>
<li>Pain during sex</li>
<li>Fever, sometimes with chills</li>
<li>Painful, frequent or difficult urination</li>
</ul>
<p>Causes</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Many types of bacteria can cause PID, but gonorrhea or chlamydia infections are the most common. These bacteria are usually acquired during unprotected sex.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Less commonly, bacteria can enter your reproductive tract anytime the normal barrier created by the cervix is disturbed. This can happen during menstruation and after childbirth, miscarriage or abortion.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Rarely, bacteria can also enter the reproductive tract during the insertion of an intrauterine device (IUD) — a form of long-term birth control — or any medical procedure that involves inserting instruments into the uterus.</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>Risk factors</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>A number of factors might increase your risk of pelvic inflammatory disease, including:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Being sexually active and younger than 25 years old</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Having multiple sexual partners</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Being in a sexual relationship with someone who has more than one sex partner</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Having sex without a condom</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Having a history of pelvic inflammatory disease or a sexually transmitted infection</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Ectopic pregnancy. PID is a major cause of tubal (ectopic) pregnancy. An ectopic pregnancy can occur when untreated PID has caused scar tissue to develop in the fallopian tubes. The scar tissue prevents the fertilized egg from making its way through the fallopian tube to implant in the uterus. Instead, the egg implants in the fallopian tube. Ectopic pregnancies can cause massive, life-threatening bleeding and require emergency medical attention.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Infertility. Damage to your reproductive organs may cause infertility — the inability to become pregnant. The more times you've had PID, the greater your risk of infertility. Delaying treatment for PID also dramatically increases your risk of infertility.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Chronic pelvic pain. Pelvic inflammatory disease can cause pelvic pain that might last for months or years. Scarring in your fallopian tubes and other pelvic organs can cause pain during intercourse and ovulation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Tubo-ovarian abscess. PID might cause an abscess — a collection of pus — to form in your reproductive tract. Most commonly, abscesses affect the fallopian tubes and ovaries, but they can also develop in the uterus or in other pelvic organs. If an abscess is left untreated, you could develop a life-threatening infection.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>medical history. Your provider will likely ask about your sexual habits, history of sexually transmitted infections and method of birth control.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Signs and symptoms.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A pelvic exam. During the exam, the health care provider will check your pelvic region for tenderness and swelling. The health care provider may also use cotton swabs to take fluid samples from your vagina and cervix. The samples will be tested at a lab for signs of infection and organisms such as gonorrhea and chlamydia.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Blood and urine tests. These tests may be used to test for pregnancy, human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) or other sexually transmitted infections, or to measure white blood cell counts or other markers of infection or inflammation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ultrasound. This test uses sound waves to create images of your reproductive organs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the diagnosis is still unclear, you may need additional tests, such as:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Laparoscopy. During this procedure, the health care provider inserts a thin, lighted instrument through a small incision in your abdomen to view your pelvic organs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Endometrial biopsy. During this procedure, the health care provider inserts a thin tube into the uterus to remove a small sample of endometrial tissue. The tissue is tested for signs of infection and inflammation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Read on the management of PID</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nutritional-and-metabolic-disorders">Nutritional and metabolic disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nutritional-and-metabolic-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Rickets</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>is a condition that affects bone development in children. It causes bone pain, poor growth and soft, weak bones that can lead to bone deformities. Adults can experience a similar condition, which is known as osteomalacia or soft bones.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Rickets is a skeletal disorder that's caused by a lack of vitamin D, calcium, or phosphate. These nutrients are important for the development of strong, healthy bones. People with rickets may have weak and soft bones, stunted growth, and, in severe cases, skeletal deformities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>NB: Rickets is most common in children who are between 6 and 36 months old. Children are at the highest risk of rickets because they’re still growing</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="what-causes-rickets">What causes rickets?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-causes-rickets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Nutrition problems or genetics are usually the cause of rickets.</li>
<li>Nutritional rickets: This is usually caused by not taking in enough vitamin D, which your body uses to absorb calcium. Some of the causes of this deficiency include:</li>
<li>Insufficient exposure to sunlight (which helps your body make its own vitamin D).</li>
<li>Lack of vitamin D in your child’s diet. This can happen with vegetarian/vegan diets, lactose intolerance or conditions that limit how much vitamin D your child’s body can absorb (such as Crohn’s disease, celiac disease, cystic fibrosis or ulcerative colitis).</li>
<li>A diet very low in calcium.</li>
<li>Inherited rickets: Several genetic diseases interfere with how your child’s body absorbs vitamin D. Other genetic conditions affect how your body handles phosphorus and also cause rickets.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="what-are-the-symptoms-of-rickets">What are the symptoms of rickets?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-the-symptoms-of-rickets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Symptoms of rickets include:</li>
<li>pain or tenderness in the bones of the arms, legs, pelvis, or spine</li>
<li>stunted growth and short stature</li>
<li>bone fractures</li>
<li>muscle cramps</li>
<li>teeth deformities, such as:</li>
<li>delayed tooth formation</li>
<li>holes in the enamel</li>
<li>abscesses</li>
<li>defects in the tooth structure</li>
<li>an increased number of cavities</li>
<li>skeletal deformities, including:</li>
<li>an oddly shaped skull</li>
<li>bowlegs, or legs that bow out</li>
<li>bumps in the ribcage</li>
<li>a protruding breastbone</li>
<li>a curved spine</li>
<li>pelvic deformities</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-is-rickets-diagnosed">How is rickets diagnosed?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-is-rickets-diagnosed" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>blood tests to measure the levels of calcium and phosphate in the blood</li>
<li>bone X-rays to check for bone deformities</li>
</ul>
<p>Management and Treatment</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Diet changes. This usually involves high doses of vitamin D, from either diet or supplements. These doses may be given for several months, depending on the severity of the case and other factors. Your pediatrician may also recommend a standard daily vitamin D supplement once the higher doses aren’t needed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Sunlight. Because your body can naturally make vitamin D when you’ve been exposed to sunlight, encouragement to get outside and get some sunshine is likely.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Surgery. Usually, your child’s bones will straighten out on their own. For especially severe cases, children may need to wear braces to help correct the bending of their bones. In some cases, surgery may also be an option.</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="osteomalacia">OSTEOMALACIA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osteomalacia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>softening of the bones. It most often occurs because of a problem with vitamin D, which helps your body absorb calcium. Your body needs calcium to maintain the strength and hardness of your bones.</li>
<li>The most common cause is not having enough vitamin D.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-fc53a674.jpeg" alt="img-118.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathophysiology">Pathophysiology:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathophysiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-3aa79f4c.jpeg" alt="img-119.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="causes">Causes:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Insufficient calcium absorption from the intestine—lack of calcium or resistance to the action of vitamin D</li>
<li>Increased renal phosphorus level</li>
<li>Under nutrition during pregnancy</li>
<li>Malabsorption Syndrome</li>
<li>Partial Gastrectomy</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="risk-factors">Risk factors:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Dietary deficiency (dietary, sunlight)</li>
<li>Sunlight exposure inadequate</li>
<li>Malabsorption- celiac disease, chronic biliary tract obstruction, small bowel resection</li>
<li>Severe renal insufficiency, liver disease</li>
<li>Prolonged anticonvulsant therapy</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-manifestations">Clinical manifestations:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-manifestations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Bone pain, tenderness and fracture</li>
<li>Muscle weakness, waddling(unusual) or limping gait</li>
<li>Difficulty in climbing up and down stairs.</li>
<li>Tetany may manifest as a carpopedal spasm and facial twitching</li>
<li>Kyphosis, rib deformities</li>
<li>Marked bowing of the tibia and femurs</li>
<li>Delayed healing or poor retention of internal fixation devices</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>X-ray- transverse line or pseudofractures (Looser's Zone i.e pseudo fracture at the stress point common at pubic ramii, axillary boarder of scapula, ribs, medial cortex of the neck of femur).</li>
<li>Bone biopsy (Excess uncalcified osteoid)</li>
<li>Laboratory test- urine, blood ( Low Calcium, Low Phosphate, Alkaline phosphatase high)</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment of osteomalacia:</p>
<p>Treatment depends on causes, signs and symptoms</p>
<ul>
<li>Exercise</li>
<li>Calcium and vitamin D(400 IU daily) supplement</li>
<li>Regular Blood test</li>
</ul>
<p>Sources of Vit. D</p>
<p>✓ Sunlight: 15 min. a day is sufficient or alternatively, 5-15 min to sun exposure at least twice weekly.
✓ Food: Vitamin fortified food products like Milk, orange Juice and cereals, fatty fish, egg yolk, liver.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="recommended-vit-d">Recommended Vit.D<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recommended-vit-d" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age</th><th>Male</th><th>Female</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>0-12 months</td><td>400 IU (10mcg)</td><td>400 IU (10mcg)</td></tr><tr><td>1-13 years</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td></tr><tr><td>14-18 years</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td></tr><tr><td>19-50 years</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td></tr><tr><td>51-70 years</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td><td>600 IU (15mcg)</td></tr><tr><td>More than 70 years</td><td>800 IU (20mcg)</td><td>800 IU (20mcg)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prognosis">Prognosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prognosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Improvement can be seen within a few weeks in some people with vitamin deficiency disorders.</li>
<li>Complete healing with treatment takes place in 6 months.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Diet rich in Vitamin D</li>
<li>Get sunlight</li>
</ul>
<p>Nursing management:</p>
<ol>
<li>Describe disease process and treatment regimen</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Describe specific factors contributing to disease process</li>
<li>Consumes therapeutic amounts of calcium and vitamin D</li>
<li>Exposes self to sunlight</li>
<li>Monitors serum calcium level regularly</li>
<li>Keeps follow-up visit routinely and as necessary</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Relieve pain-physical, psychologic and pharmaceutical measures</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Handle gently while assisting patient and avoid unnecessary and frequent movement</li>
<li>Advice or use a convoluted foam mattress and soft pillow</li>
<li>Use diversional activities</li>
<li>Provide analgesic according to prescription</li>
<li>Monitor effects of interventions/pain relief</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="food-sources-of-vit-d">Food sources of Vit D<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#food-sources-of-vit-d" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>1</th><th>Sources</th><th>IU /serving</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>2</td><td>Egg 1 large (Yolk)</td><td>41</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>Cereals 0.75-1 cup</td><td>40</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>Milk non fat 1 cup</td><td>115-124</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>Yogurt (6ounce)</td><td>80</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>Orange juice 1 cup</td><td>137</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>Tuna fish canned in water, drained 3 ounce</td><td>154</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="osteoporosis">Osteoporosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osteoporosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="osteoporosis">Osteoporosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osteoporosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="definition">Definition:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>It is a disease where</p>
<ul>
<li>decrease in the density of bone</li>
<li>decreasing its strength and</li>
<li>resulting in fragile bones.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-ad6ddf1d.jpeg" alt="img-120.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-85bae4b5.jpeg" alt="img-121.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification-of-osteoporosis">Classification of Osteoporosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-osteoporosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="primary-osteoporosis">Primary osteoporosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-osteoporosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Juvenile osteoporosis.</li>
<li>Idiopathic osteoporosis</li>
<li>Postmenopausal osteoporosis</li>
<li>Age-related, or senile, osteoporosis</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="secondary-osteoporosis">Secondary osteoporosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#secondary-osteoporosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Congenital</li>
<li>Diet</li>
<li>Drugs</li>
<li>Endocrine disorder</li>
<li>Other Systemic Disorder</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-582cc65f.jpeg" alt="img-122.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="risk-factors-of-osteoporosis">Risk factors of Osteoporosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors-of-osteoporosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-92c8f85a.jpeg" alt="img-123.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="risk-factors">Risk factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-da1af5d0.jpeg" alt="img-124.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="being-female">Being Female<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#being-female" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>With the onset of
menopause (mid-forties
or fifties), diminishing
estrogen levels lead to
excessive bone
resorption that is not
fully compensated by
an increase in bone
formation</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-a6b66196.jpeg" alt="img-125.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="risk-factors">Risk factors...<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Being female</li>
<li>Older age</li>
<li>Family history of osteoporosis.</li>
<li>History of broken bones</li>
<li>Low sex hormones</li>
<li>Low estrogen levels in women, including menopause</li>
<li>Missing periods (amenorrhea)</li>
<li>Low levels of testosterone in male.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-250a2258.jpeg" alt="img-126.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="8d2d2da">Risk factors…<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8d2d2da" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-3edf94c4.jpeg" alt="img-127.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Diet</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Low calcium intake</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Low vitamin D intake</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Excessive intake of protein, sodium and caffeine</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Inactive lifestyle</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Smoking, Alcohol abuse</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-69a3f568.jpeg" alt="img-128.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="risk-factors">Risk factors...<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Certain medications</li>
<li>steroid, anticonvulsants etc</li>
<li>Certain diseases</li>
<li>anorexia nervosa, rheumatoid arthritis, gastrointestinal diseases and others</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-b2bebea8.jpeg" alt="img-129.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-bec3dfea.jpeg" alt="img-130.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-does-osteoporosis-presents">How does Osteoporosis presents?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-does-osteoporosis-presents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-592931f1.jpeg" alt="img-131.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="presentations">presentations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presentations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>People may not know that they have osteoporosis until they break a bone.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-6ae063ed.jpeg" alt="img-132.jpeg"></p>
<p>Compression
Fracture</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="presentations">presentations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presentations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-3b680e43.jpeg" alt="img-133.jpeg"></p>
<p>Persistent, unexplained back pain</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="9644e2b2">Presentations…<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9644e2b2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Vertebral (spinal) fractures may initially be felt or seen in the form of</li>
<li>Persistent, unexplained back pain</li>
<li>Loss of height</li>
<li>Spinal deformities such as kyphosis or stooped posture.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-86221b89.jpeg" alt="img-134.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-252063f3.jpeg" alt="img-135.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="cnt">CNT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cnt" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Prevention</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>adequate amounts of calcium</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>vitamin D</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>exercise regularly</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Diagnosis</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>history and physical examination</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>skeletal x-rays</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>bone densitometry</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>specialized laboratory tests to check for ostemalacia, hyperparathyroidism</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-eac6554a.jpeg" alt="img-136.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>FRACTURE,</p>
<p>The most serious complication of Osteoporosis that leads to</p>
<ul>
<li>Increased morbidity</li>
<li>Increased mortality</li>
<li>Decreased quality of life</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-7e165f28.jpeg" alt="img-137.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-53209413.jpeg" alt="img-138.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="goals-of-management">Goals of management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#goals-of-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-42be36de.jpeg" alt="img-139.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>Prevent further bone loss</li>
<li>Increase or at least stabilize bone density.</li>
<li>Relieve pain and prevent fracture.</li>
<li>Increase level of physical functioning</li>
<li>Increase quality of life</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-of-complications">Prevention of complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-of-complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-71c6ba23.jpeg" alt="img-140.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>Exercise/activity programs to improve strength and endurance</li>
<li>Gait training</li>
<li>Awareness creation to prevent slipping</li>
<li>Regular medical check-up</li>
<li>Treat medical conditions, e.g., as postural hypotension, anemia, dementia</li>
<li>Alarm systems, assistive devices</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="gout">GOUT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#gout" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="definition">DEFINITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-2ee7ff7a.jpeg" alt="img-141.jpeg"></p>
<p>It is derived from the Latin word <strong>gutta</strong>, meaning "a drop" (of liquid).</p>
<p>Gout is a metabolic disorder characterized by elevated serum uric acid levels and deposits of urate crystals in synovial fluids and surrounding tissues.</p>
<p>Gout also is a kind of arthritis that occurs when <strong>uric acid</strong> builds up in blood and causes joint inflammation.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-33afb45f.jpeg" alt="img-142.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-dfb44e17.jpeg" alt="img-143.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-0616d88d.jpeg" alt="img-144.jpeg"></p>
<p>Lifestyle</p>
<p>Medical conditions</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-46270451.jpeg" alt="img-145.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-1923cb5b.jpeg" alt="img-146.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-6daa7bd5.jpeg" alt="img-147.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-2e3b4436.jpeg" alt="img-148.jpeg"></p>
<p>Severe pain at the joint
Itchy and peeling skin later
Redness and Inflammation
Fever
Less Flexibility
Becomes Nodules</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-e6d54083.jpeg" alt="img-149.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-34391b45.jpeg" alt="img-150.jpeg"></p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="etiology">ETIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Classification causes of Gout</p>
<p>Primary Gout (Hereditary)</p>
<p>Secondary Gout (internal/external factors, e.g. diet, kidney problems, obesity)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="etiology-cont">Etiology cOnt.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology-cont" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-63f7b0b6.jpeg" alt="img-155.jpeg"></p>
<p>A high dietary intake of purine, alcohol intake</p>
<p>increased purine synthesis, decreased renal excretion</p>
<p>PATHOPHYSIOLOGY</p>
<p>Increase uric acid in the blood (hyperuricemia)</p>
<p>Deposition of uric acid into the joints</p>
<p>GOUT</p>
